<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Law</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">System of rules and guidelines, generally backed by governmental authority</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Law_(disambiguation)" title="Law (disambiguation)">Law (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Legal" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Legal_(disambiguation)" title="Legal (disambiguation)">Legal (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Justice-2060093_1920.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Justice-2060093_1920.jpg/220px-Justice-2060093_1920.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Justice-2060093_1920.jpg/330px-Justice-2060093_1920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Justice-2060093_1920.jpg/440px-Justice-2060093_1920.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Justice-2060093_1920.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iustitia ("<a href="/wiki/Lady_Justice" title="Lady Justice">Lady Justice</a>") is a symbolic personification of the coercive power of a <a href="/wiki/Tribunal" title="Tribunal">tribunal</a>: a sword representing state authority, <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale#Balance" title="Weighing scale">scales</a> representing an objective standard and a blindfold indicating that justice should be impartial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><b>Law</b> is a set of rules that are created and are <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement" title="Law enforcement">enforceable</a> by social or governmental institutions to regulate behavior,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-2">[2]</a></sup> with its precise definition a matter of longstanding debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-willis_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-willis-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpgibbs_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpgibbs-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-akers_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-akers-5">[5]</a></sup> It has been variously described as a <a href="/wiki/Social_science#Law" title="Social science">science</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaquero_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaquero-7">[7]</a></sup> and as the art of justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mlcohen_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-mlcohen-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> State-enforced laws can be made by a group <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislature</a> or by a single legislator, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Statute" title="Statute">statutes</a>; by the executive through <a href="/wiki/Decree" title="Decree">decrees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Regulation" title="Regulation">regulations</a>; or established by judges through <a href="/wiki/Precedent" title="Precedent">precedent</a>, usually in <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> jurisdictions. Private individuals may create legally binding <a href="/wiki/Contract" title="Contract">contracts</a>, including arbitration agreements that adopt <a href="/wiki/Alternative_dispute_resolution" title="Alternative dispute resolution">alternative ways of resolving disputes</a> to standard court litigation. The creation of laws themselves may be influenced by a <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitution</a>, written or tacit, and the <a href="/wiki/Rights" title="Rights">rights</a> encoded therein. The law shapes <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a> and <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a> in various ways and serves as a mediator of relations between people.
</p><p>Legal systems vary between <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction_(area)" title="Jurisdiction (area)">jurisdictions</a>, with their differences analysed in <a href="/wiki/Comparative_law" title="Comparative law">comparative law</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> jurisdictions, a legislature or other central body <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codifies and consolidates</a> the law. In <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> systems, judges may make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legally_binding" title="Legally binding">binding</a> case law through <a href="/wiki/Precedent" title="Precedent">precedent</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> although on occasion this may be overturned by a higher court or the legislature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Historically, <a href="/wiki/Religious_law" title="Religious law">religious law</a> has influenced secular matters <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and is, as of the 21st century, still in use in some religious communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharia_law" title="Sharia law">Sharia law</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> principles is used as the primary legal system in several countries, including <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sharia-inc_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-sharia-inc-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lex_Heraldry.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="772" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lex_Heraldry.svg/220px-Lex_Heraldry.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lex_Heraldry.svg/330px-Lex_Heraldry.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Lex_Heraldry.svg/440px-Lex_Heraldry.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lex_Heraldry.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Classic symbol of law in heraldry</div></div></div>
<p>The scope of law can be divided into two domains. <a href="/wiki/Public_law" title="Public law">Public law</a> concerns government and society, including <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_law" title="Constitutional law">constitutional law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Administrative_law" title="Administrative law">administrative law</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law" title="Criminal law">criminal law</a>. <a href="/wiki/Private_law" title="Private law">Private law</a> deals with legal disputes between individuals and/or organisations in areas such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contracts" title="Contracts">contracts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">property</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Torts" title="Torts">torts</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delicts" title="Delicts">delicts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commercial_law" title="Commercial law">commercial law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horwitz-pp_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-horwitz-pp-18">[18]</a></sup> This distinction is stronger in <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> countries, particularly those with a separate system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Administrative_courts" title="Administrative courts">administrative courts</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merryman_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-merryman-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> by contrast, the public-private law divide is less pronounced in <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> jurisdictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harlow_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-harlow-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Law provides a source of scholarly inquiry into <a href="/wiki/Legal_history" title="Legal history">legal history</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_analysis_of_law" title="Economic analysis of law">economic analysis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rubin_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-rubin-25">[25]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sociology_of_law" title="Sociology of law">sociology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-law+sociology_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-law+sociology-26">[26]</a></sup> Law also raises important and complex issues concerning equality, fairness, and <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pound1914_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-pound1914-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>

</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_of_law">Philosophy of law</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">Jurisprudence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_law" title="Philosophy of law">Philosophy of law</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:22em; ; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>But what, after all, is a law? [...] When I say that the object of laws is always general, I mean that law considers subjects en masse and actions in the abstract, and never a particular person or action. [...] On this view, we at once see that it can no longer be asked whose business it is to make laws, since they are acts of the <a href="/wiki/General_will" title="General will">general will</a>; nor whether the prince is above the law, since he is a member of the State; nor whether the law can be unjust, since no one is unjust to himself; nor how we can be both free and subject to the laws, since they are but registers of our wills.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">Jean-Jacques Rousseau, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Social_Contract" title="The Social Contract">The Social Contract</a></i>, II, 6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The philosophy of law is commonly known as jurisprudence. Normative jurisprudence asks "what should law be?", while analytic jurisprudence asks "what is law?"
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Analytical_jurisprudence">Analytical jurisprudence</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Analytical_jurisprudence" title="Analytical jurisprudence">Analytical jurisprudence</a></div>
<p>There have been several attempts to produce "a universally acceptable definition of law". In 1972, <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Lloyd,_Baron_Lloyd_of_Hampstead" title="Dennis Lloyd, Baron Lloyd of Hampstead">Baron Hampstead</a> suggested that no such definition could be produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jurisprudence._Third_Edition_1972._Page_39_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jurisprudence._Third_Edition_1972._Page_39-30">[30]</a></sup> McCoubrey and White said that the question "what is law?" has no simple answer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glanville_Williams" title="Glanville Williams">Glanville Williams</a> said that the meaning of the word "law" depends on the context in which that word is used. He said that, for example, "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_customary_law" title="Early customary law">early customary law</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Municipal_law" title="Municipal law">municipal law</a>" were contexts where the word "law" had two different and irreconcilable meanings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thurman_Arnold" title="Thurman Arnold">Thurman Arnold</a> said that it is obvious that it is impossible to define the word "law" and that it is also equally obvious that the struggle to define that word should not ever be abandoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> It is possible to take the view that there is no need to define the word "law" (e.g. "let's forget about generalities and get down to <a href="/wiki/Case_law" title="Case law">cases</a>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>One definition is that law is a system of rules and guidelines which are enforced through social institutions to govern behaviour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-2">[2]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Concept_of_Law" title="The Concept of Law">The Concept of Law</a>,</i> <a href="/wiki/H._L._A._Hart" title="H. L. A. Hart">H.L.A Hart</a> argued that law is a "system of rules";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Austin_(legal_philosopher)" title="John Austin (legal philosopher)">John Austin</a> said law was "the command of a sovereign, backed by the threat of a sanction";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jap_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-jap-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Dworkin" title="Ronald Dworkin">Ronald Dworkin</a> describes law as an "interpretive concept" to achieve <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a> in his text titled <i><a href="/wiki/Law%27s_Empire" title="Law's Empire">Law's Empire</a></i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dworkin,_[[Law's_Empire]],_410_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dworkin,_[[Law's_Empire]],_410-37">[37]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Raz" title="Joseph Raz">Joseph Raz</a> argues law is an "authority" to mediate people's interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jra_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-jra-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Wendell_Holmes_Jr." title="Oliver Wendell Holmes Jr.">Oliver Wendell Holmes</a> said, "The prophecies of what the courts will do in fact, and nothing more pretentious, are what I mean by the law."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In his <i><a href="/wiki/Treatise_on_Law" title="Treatise on Law">Treatise on Law</a>,</i> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> argues that law is a rational ordering of things which concern the common good that is promulgated by whoever is charged with the care of the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> This definition has both <a href="/wiki/Legal_positivism" title="Legal positivism">positivist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">naturalist</a> elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Connection_to_morality_and_justice">Connection to morality and justice</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Rule_according_to_higher_law" title="Rule according to higher law">Rule according to higher law</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Laki-patsas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5616" data-file-width="3744" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Laki-patsas.jpg/220px-Laki-patsas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Laki-patsas.jpg/330px-Laki-patsas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Laki-patsas.jpg/440px-Laki-patsas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Laki-patsas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The Law" sculpture at interior of the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Palace,_Helsinki" title="Presidential Palace, Helsinki">Presidential Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Definitions of law often raise the question of the extent to which law incorporates morality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twtaylor_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-twtaylor-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Austin_(legal_philosopher)" title="John Austin (legal philosopher)">John Austin</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarian</a> answer was that law is "commands, backed by threat of sanctions, from a sovereign, to whom people have a habit of obedience".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jap_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-jap-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">Natural lawyers</a> on the other side, such as <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, argue that law reflects essentially moral and unchangeable laws of nature. The concept of "natural law" emerged in ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">Greek philosophy</a> concurrently and in connection with the notion of justice, and re-entered the mainstream of <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a> through the writings of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, notably his <i><a href="/wiki/Treatise_on_Law" title="Treatise on Law">Treatise on Law</a></i>.
</p><p>When having completed the first two parts of his book <i><a href="/wiki/Splendeurs_et_mis%C3%A8res_des_courtisanes" title="Splendeurs et misères des courtisanes">Splendeurs et misères des courtisanes</a></i>, which he intended to be the end of the entire work, <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_de_Balzac" title="Honoré de Balzac">Honoré de Balzac</a> visited the <a href="/wiki/Conciergerie" title="Conciergerie">Conciergerie</a>. Thereafter, he decided to add a third part, finally named <i>Où mènent les mauvais chemins</i> (<i>The Ends of Evil Ways</i>), entirely dedicated to describing the conditions in prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pleiade_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pleiade-43">[43]</a></sup> In this third part, he states:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The law is good, it is necessary, its execution is poor, and the manners judge the laws based on the manner in which they are executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a>, the founder of a purely rationalistic system of natural law, argued that law arises from both a social impulse—as Aristotle had indicated—and reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> believed a moral imperative requires laws "be chosen as though they should hold as universal laws of nature".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a> and his student Austin, following <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, believed that this conflated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Is-ought_problem" title="Is-ought problem">"is" and what "ought to be"</a> problem. Bentham and Austin argued for law's <a href="/wiki/Legal_positivism" title="Legal positivism">positivism</a>; that real law is entirely separate from "morality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Kant was also criticised by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a>, who rejected the principle of equality, and believed that law emanates from the <a href="/wiki/Will_to_power" title="Will to power">will to power</a>, and cannot be labeled as "moral" or "immoral".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1934, the Austrian philosopher <a href="/wiki/Hans_Kelsen" title="Hans Kelsen">Hans Kelsen</a> continued the positivist tradition in his book the <i><a href="/wiki/Pure_Theory_of_Law" title="Pure Theory of Law">Pure Theory of Law</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Kelsen believed that although law is separate from morality, it is endowed with "normativity", meaning we ought to obey it. While laws are positive "is" statements (e.g. the fine for reversing on a highway <i>is</i> €500); law tells us what we "should" do. Thus, each legal system can be hypothesised to have a basic norm (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grundnorm" title="Grundnorm">Grundnorm</a></i>) instructing us to obey. Kelsen's major opponent, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schmitt" title="Carl Schmitt">Carl Schmitt</a>, rejected both positivism and the idea of the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a> because he did not accept the primacy of abstract normative principles over concrete political positions and decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Therefore, Schmitt advocated a jurisprudence of the exception (<a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">state of emergency</a>), which denied that legal norms could encompass all of the political experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-F171_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-F171-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="728" data-file-width="536" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg/220px-Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg/330px-Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg/440px-Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jeremy_Bentham_by_Henry_William_Pickersgill_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bentham's utilitarian theories remained dominant in law until the 20th century.</div></div></div>
<p>Later in the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/H._L._A._Hart" title="H. L. A. Hart">H. L. A. Hart</a> attacked Austin for his simplifications and Kelsen for his fictions in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Concept_of_Law" title="The Concept of Law">The Concept of Law</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Hart argued law is a system of rules, divided into primary (rules of conduct) and secondary ones (rules addressed to officials to administer primary rules). Secondary rules are further divided into rules of adjudication (to resolve legal disputes), rules of change (allowing laws to be varied) and the rule of recognition (allowing laws to be identified as valid). Two of Hart's students continued the debate: In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Law%27s_Empire" title="Law's Empire">Law's Empire</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Dworkin" title="Ronald Dworkin">Ronald Dworkin</a> attacked Hart and the positivists for their refusal to treat law as a moral issue. Dworkin argues that law is an "<a href="/wiki/Interpretivism_(legal)" title="Interpretivism (legal)">interpretive</a> concept",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dworkin,_[[Law's_Empire]],_410_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dworkin,_[[Law's_Empire]],_410-37">[37]</a></sup> that requires judges to find the best fitting and most just solution to a legal dispute, given their constitutional traditions. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Raz" title="Joseph Raz">Joseph Raz</a>, on the other hand, defended the positivist outlook and criticised Hart's "soft social thesis" approach in <i>The Authority of Law</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jra_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-jra-38">[38]</a></sup> Raz argues that law is authority, identifiable purely through social sources and without reference to moral reasoning. In his view, any categorisation of rules beyond their role as authoritative instruments in mediation are best left to <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>, rather than jurisprudence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Legal_history" title="Legal history">Legal history</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milkau_Oberer_Teil_der_Stele_mit_dem_Text_von_Hammurapis_Gesetzescode_369-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="714" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Milkau_Oberer_Teil_der_Stele_mit_dem_Text_von_Hammurapis_Gesetzescode_369-2.jpg/220px-Milkau_Oberer_Teil_der_Stele_mit_dem_Text_von_Hammurapis_Gesetzescode_369-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Milkau_Oberer_Teil_der_Stele_mit_dem_Text_von_Hammurapis_Gesetzescode_369-2.jpg/330px-Milkau_Oberer_Teil_der_Stele_mit_dem_Text_von_Hammurapis_Gesetzescode_369-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Milkau_Oberer_Teil_der_Stele_mit_dem_Text_von_Hammurapis_Gesetzescode_369-2.jpg/440px-Milkau_Oberer_Teil_der_Stele_mit_dem_Text_von_Hammurapis_Gesetzescode_369-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milkau_Oberer_Teil_der_Stele_mit_dem_Text_von_Hammurapis_Gesetzescode_369-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King <a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">Hammurabi</a> is revealed the <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">code of laws</a> by the Mesopotamian sun god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">Shamash</a>, also revered as the god of justice.</div></div></div>
<p>The history of law links closely to the development of <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptian</a> law, dating as far back as 3000 BC, was based on the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ma%27at" title="Ma'at">Ma'at</a> and characterised by tradition, <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetorical</a> speech, social equality and impartiality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> By the 22nd century BC, the ancient <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Ur-Nammu" title="Ur-Nammu">Ur-Nammu</a> had formulated the first <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Ur-Nammu" title="Code of Ur-Nammu">law code</a>, which consisted of <a href="/wiki/Casuistry" title="Casuistry">casuistic</a> statements ("if … then ..."). Around 1760 BC, <a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">King Hammurabi</a> further developed <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_law" title="Babylonian law">Babylonian law</a>, by codifying and inscribing it in stone. Hammurabi placed several copies of his law code throughout the kingdom of Babylon as <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stelae</a>, for the entire public to see; this became known as the <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">Codex Hammurabi</a>. The most intact copy of these stelae was discovered in the 19th century by British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyriologists" title="Assyriologists">Assyriologists</a>, and has since been fully <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transliterated" title="Transliterated">transliterated</a> and translated into various languages, including English, Italian, German, and French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> dates back to 1280 BC and takes the form of moral imperatives as recommendations for a good society. The small <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> city-state, ancient <a href="/wiki/History_of_Athens" title="History of Athens">Athens</a>, from about the 8th century BC was the first society to be based on broad inclusion of its citizenry, excluding women and <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_ancient_Greece" title="Slavery in ancient Greece">enslaved people</a>. However, Athens had no legal science or single word for "law",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> relying instead on the three-way distinction between divine law (<i>thémis</i>), human decree (<i>nomos</i>) and custom (<i>díkē</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Yet <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_law" title="Ancient Greek law">Ancient Greek law</a> contained major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solonian_Constitution" title="Solonian Constitution">constitutional</a> innovations in the development of <a href="/wiki/Athenian_democracy" title="Athenian democracy">democracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a> was heavily influenced by Greek philosophy, but its detailed rules were developed by professional jurists and were highly sophisticated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Over the centuries between the rise and decline of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, law was adapted to cope with the changing social situations and underwent major codification under <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-St107_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-St107-65">[65]</a></sup> Although codes were replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Custom_(law)" title="Custom (law)">custom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Case_law" title="Case law">case law</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, Roman law was rediscovered around the 11th century when medieval legal scholars began to research Roman codes and adapt their concepts to the <a href="/wiki/Canon_law_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Canon law of the Catholic Church">canon law</a>, giving birth to the <i><a href="/wiki/Jus_commune" title="Jus commune">jus commune</a></i>. Latin <a href="/wiki/Legal_maxim" title="Legal maxim">legal maxims</a> (called <a href="/wiki/Brocard_(law)" title="Brocard (law)">brocards</a>) were compiled for guidance. In medieval England, royal courts developed a body of <a href="/wiki/Precedent" title="Precedent">precedent</a> which later became the <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>. A Europe-wide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_Merchant" title="Law Merchant">Law Merchant</a> was formed so that merchants could trade with common standards of practice rather than with the many splintered facets of local laws. The Law Merchant, a precursor to modern commercial law, emphasised the freedom to contract and alienability of property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a> grew in the 18th and 19th centuries, the Law Merchant was incorporated into countries' local law under new civil codes. The <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic</a> and <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BCrgerliches_Gesetzbuch" title="Bürgerliches Gesetzbuch">German</a> Codes became the most influential. In contrast to English common law, which consists of enormous tomes of case law, codes in small books are easy to export and easy for judges to apply. However, today there are signs that civil and common law are converging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mattei71_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-mattei71-67">[67]</a></sup> EU law is codified in treaties, but develops through <i>de facto</i> precedent laid down by the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">European Court of Justice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Constitution_of_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3509" data-file-width="2481" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Constitution_of_India.jpg/220px-Constitution_of_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Constitution_of_India.jpg/330px-Constitution_of_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Constitution_of_India.jpg/440px-Constitution_of_India.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Constitution_of_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_India" title="Constitution of India">Constitution of India</a> is the longest written constitution for a country, containing 444 articles, 12 schedules, numerous amendments and 117,369 words.</div></div></div>
<p>Ancient <a href="/wiki/Law_of_India" title="Law of India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_law" title="Chinese law">China</a> represent distinct traditions of law, and have historically had independent schools of legal theory and practice. The <i><a href="/wiki/Arthashastra" title="Arthashastra">Arthashastra</a></i>, probably compiled around 100 AD (although it contains older material), and the <i><a href="/wiki/Manusmriti" title="Manusmriti">Manusmriti</a></i> (c. 100–300 AD) were foundational treatises in India, and comprise texts considered authoritative legal guidance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Manu's central philosophy was tolerance and <a href="/wiki/Pluralism_(political_philosophy)" title="Pluralism (political philosophy)">pluralism</a>, and was cited across Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="Muslim conquests in the Indian subcontinent">Muslim conquests in the Indian subcontinent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a> was established by the Muslim sultanates and empires, most notably <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatawa-e-Alamgiri" title="Fatawa-e-Alamgiri">Fatawa-e-Alamgiri</a>, compiled by emperor <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a> and various scholars of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In India, the <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> legal tradition, along with Islamic law, were both supplanted by common law when India <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">became part</a> of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Malaysia, Brunei, <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Singapore" title="Law of Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Hong_Kong" title="Law of Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> also adopted the common law system. The eastern Asia legal tradition reflects a unique blend of secular and religious influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Japan was the first country to begin modernising its legal system along western lines, by importing parts of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_Civil" title="Code Civil">French</a>, but mostly the German Civil Code.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> This partly reflected Germany's status as a rising power in the late 19th century. Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_law" title="Traditional Chinese law">traditional Chinese law</a> gave way to westernisation towards the final years of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing Dynasty</a> in the form of six private law codes based mainly on the Japanese model of German law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Today <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="Law of the Republic of China">Taiwanese law</a> retains the closest affinity to the codifications from that period, because of the split between <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a>'s nationalists, who fled there, and <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a>'s communists who won control of the mainland in 1949. The current legal infrastructure in the People's Republic of China was heavily influenced by <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> <a href="/wiki/Socialist_law" title="Socialist law">Socialist law</a>, which essentially inflates administrative law at the expense of private law rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Due to rapid industrialisation, today China is undergoing a process of reform, at least in terms of economic, if not social and political, rights. A new contract code in 1999 represented a move away from administrative domination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Furthermore, after negotiations lasting fifteen years, in 2001 China joined the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_systems">Legal systems</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Comparative_law" title="Comparative law">Comparative law</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_legal_systems" title="List of national legal systems">List of national legal systems</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Comparative_legal_history" title="Comparative legal history">Comparative legal history</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Legal_systems_of_the_world_(en).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1870" data-file-width="3564" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Map_of_the_Legal_systems_of_the_world_%28en%29.png/220px-Map_of_the_Legal_systems_of_the_world_%28en%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Map_of_the_Legal_systems_of_the_world_%28en%29.png/330px-Map_of_the_Legal_systems_of_the_world_%28en%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Map_of_the_Legal_systems_of_the_world_%28en%29.png/440px-Map_of_the_Legal_systems_of_the_world_%28en%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Legal_systems_of_the_world_(en).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Colour-coded map of the legal systems around the world, showing civil, common law, religious, customary and mixed legal systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Common law systems are shaded pink, and civil law systems are shaded blue/turquoise.</div></div></div>
<p>In general, legal systems can be split between civil law and common law systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pejovic_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-pejovic-81">[81]</a></sup> Modern scholars argue that the significance of this distinction has progressively declined; the numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_transplants" title="Legal transplants">legal transplants</a>, typical of modern law, result in the sharing by modern legal systems of many features traditionally considered typical of either common law or civil law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mattei71_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-mattei71-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inprol-intro_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-inprol-intro-82">[82]</a></sup> The term "civil law", referring to the <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civilian legal system</a> originating in continental Europe, should not be confused with "civil law" in the sense of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(common_law)" title="Civil law (common law)">common law topics</a> distinct from <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law" title="Criminal law">criminal law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Public_law" title="Public law">public law</a>.
</p><p>The third type of legal system—accepted by some countries without <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation of church and state</a>—is religious law, based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scripture" title="Scripture">scriptures</a>. The specific system that a country is ruled by is often determined by its history, connections with other countries, or its adherence to international standards. The <a href="/wiki/Sources_of_law" title="Sources of law">sources</a> that jurisdictions adopt as authoritatively binding are the defining features of any legal system. Yet classification is a matter of form rather than substance since similar rules often prevail.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_law">Civil law</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">Civil law (legal system)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mosaic_of_Justinianus_I_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_(Ravenna).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2079" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Mosaic_of_Justinianus_I_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%29.jpg/220px-Mosaic_of_Justinianus_I_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Mosaic_of_Justinianus_I_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%29.jpg/330px-Mosaic_of_Justinianus_I_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Mosaic_of_Justinianus_I_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%29.jpg/440px-Mosaic_of_Justinianus_I_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_%28Ravenna%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mosaic_of_Justinianus_I_-_Basilica_San_Vitale_(Ravenna).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justinian" title="Justinian">Justinian</a> (527–565) of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, who ordered the codification of <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Corpus Juris Civilis</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Code_Civil_1804.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="762" data-file-width="636" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Code_Civil_1804.png/220px-Code_Civil_1804.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Code_Civil_1804.png/330px-Code_Civil_1804.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Code_Civil_1804.png/440px-Code_Civil_1804.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Code_Civil_1804.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First page of the 1804 edition of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a></div></div></div>
<p>Civil law is the legal system used in most countries around the world today. In civil law the sources recognised as authoritative are, primarily, legislation—especially <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codifications</a> in constitutions or <a href="/wiki/Statute" title="Statute">statutes</a> passed by government—and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Custom_(law)" title="Custom (law)">custom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Codifications date back millennia, with one early example being the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_law" title="Babylonian law">Babylonian</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">Codex Hammurabi</a></i>. Modern civil law systems essentially derive from legal codes issued by <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> in the 6th century, which were rediscovered by 11th century Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ-cc-diffs_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-econ-cc-diffs-84">[84]</a></sup> Roman law in the days of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> and Empire was heavily procedural, and lacked a professional legal class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Instead a lay <a href="/wiki/Magistrate" title="Magistrate">magistrate</a>, <i>iudex</i>, was chosen to adjudicate. Decisions were not published in any systematic way, so any case law that developed was disguised and almost unrecognised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Each case was to be decided afresh from the laws of the State, which mirrors the (theoretical) unimportance of judges' decisions for future cases in civil law systems today. From 529 to 534 AD the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> codified and consolidated Roman law up until that point, so that what remained was one-twentieth of the mass of legal texts from before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> This became known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Corpus Juris Civilis</a></i>. As one legal historian wrote, "Justinian consciously looked back to the golden age of Roman law and aimed to restore it to the peak it had reached three centuries before."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The Justinian Code remained in force in the East until the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. Western Europe, meanwhile, relied on a mix of the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Theodosianus" title="Codex Theodosianus">Theodosian Code</a> and Germanic customary law until the Justinian Code was rediscovered in the 11th century, and scholars at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a> used it to interpret their own laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Civil law codifications based closely on Roman law, alongside some influences from <a href="/wiki/Religious_law" title="Religious law">religious laws</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a>, continued to spread throughout Europe until the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>; then, in the 19th century, both France, with the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_Civil" title="Code Civil">Code Civil</a></i>, and Germany, with the <i><a href="/wiki/B%C3%BCrgerliches_Gesetzbuch" title="Bürgerliches Gesetzbuch">Bürgerliches Gesetzbuch</a></i>, modernised their legal codes. Both these codes influenced heavily not only the law systems of the countries in continental Europe (e.g. Greece), but also the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Japan" title="Law of Japan">Japanese</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">Korean</a> legal traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Today, countries that have civil law systems range from Russia and Turkey to most of <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anarchist_law">Anarchist law</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anarchist_law" title="Anarchist law">Anarchist law</a></div>
<p>Anarchism has been practiced in society in much of the world. Mass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist_communities" title="Anarchist communities">anarchist communities</a>, ranging from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rojava" title="Rojava">Syria</a> to the United States, exist and vary from hundreds to millions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Anarchism encompasses a broad range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist" title="Socialist">social</a> political philosophies with different tendencies and implementation.
</p><p>Anarchist law primarily deals with how anarchism is implemented upon a society, the framework based on decentralized organizations and <a href="/wiki/Mutual_aid_(organization_theory)" title="Mutual aid (organization theory)">mutual aid</a>, with representation through a form of <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a>. Laws being based upon their need.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> A large portion of anarchist ideologies such as <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-syndicalism" title="Anarcho-syndicalism">anarcho-syndicalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-communism" title="Anarcho-communism">anarcho-communism</a> primarily focuses on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decentralized" title="Decentralized">decentralized</a> worker unions, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooperatives" title="Cooperatives">cooperatives</a> and syndicates as the main instrument of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Socialist_law">Socialist law</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Socialist_law" title="Socialist law">Socialist law</a></div>
<p>Socialist law is the legal systems in <a href="/wiki/Communist_state" title="Communist state">communist states</a> such as the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-austin_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-austin-95">[95]</a></sup> Academic opinion is divided on whether it is a separate system from civil law, given major deviations based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marxist%E2%80%93Leninist" title="Marxist–Leninist">Marxist–Leninist</a> ideology, such as subordinating the judiciary to the executive ruling party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-austin_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-austin-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gbsmith_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-gbsmith-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_law_and_equity">Common law and equity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">Common law</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joao_sem_terra_assina_carta_Magna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="395" decoding="async" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Joao_sem_terra_assina_carta_Magna.jpg/220px-Joao_sem_terra_assina_carta_Magna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Joao_sem_terra_assina_carta_Magna.jpg/330px-Joao_sem_terra_assina_carta_Magna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/Joao_sem_terra_assina_carta_Magna.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joao_sem_terra_assina_carta_Magna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King John of England signs Magna Carta.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Common_law#1._Common_law_as_opposed_to_statutory_law_and_regulatory_law" title="Common law">common law</a> legal systems, decisions by courts are explicitly acknowledged as "law" on equal footing with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statute_law" title="Statute law">statutes</a> adopted through the legislative process and with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regulation_(law)" title="Regulation (law)">regulations</a> issued by the <a href="/wiki/Executive_(government)" title="Executive (government)">executive branch</a>. The "doctrine of precedent", or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stare_decisis" title="Stare decisis">stare decisis</a></i> (Latin for "to stand by decisions") means that decisions by higher courts bind lower courts, and future decisions of the same court, to assure that similar cases reach similar results. In <a href="/wiki/Common_law#2._Common_law_legal_systems_as_opposed_to_civil_law_legal_systems" title="Common law">contrast</a>, in "<a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a>" systems, legislative statutes are typically more detailed, and judicial decisions are shorter and less detailed, because the judge or barrister is only writing to decide the single case, rather than to set out reasoning that will guide future courts.
</p><p>Common law originated from England and has been inherited by almost every country once tied to the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> (except Malta, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_Scotland" title="Law of Scotland">Scotland</a>, the U.S. state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louisiana_law" title="Louisiana law">Louisiana</a>, and the Canadian province of <a href="/wiki/Quebec_law" title="Quebec law">Quebec</a>). In medieval England, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest</a> the law varied shire-to-shire, based on disparate tribal customs. The concept of a "common law" developed during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a> during the late 12th century, when Henry appointed judges that had authority to create an institutionalised and unified system of law "common" to the country. The next major step in the evolution of the common law came when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_of_England" title="John of England">King John</a> was forced by his barons to sign a document limiting his authority to pass laws. This "great charter" or <i><a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a></i> of 1215 also required that the King's entourage of judges hold their courts and judgments at "a certain place" rather than dispensing autocratic justice in unpredictable places about the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> A concentrated and elite group of judges acquired a dominant role in law-making under this system, and compared to its European counterparts the English judiciary became highly centralised. In 1297, for instance, while the highest court in France had fifty-one judges, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Court_of_Common_Pleas" title="English Court of Common Pleas">English Court of Common Pleas</a> had five.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> This powerful and tight-knit judiciary gave rise to a systematised process of developing common law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the system became overly systematised—overly rigid and inflexible. As a result, as time went on, increasing numbers of citizens petitioned the King to override the common law, and on the King's behalf the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a> gave judgment to do what was equitable in a case. From the time of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Sir Thomas More</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Lawyer" title="Lawyer">lawyer</a> to be appointed as Lord Chancellor, a systematic body of equity grew up alongside the rigid common law, and developed its own <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Chancery" title="Court of Chancery">Court of Chancery</a>. At first, equity was often criticised as erratic, that it varied according to the length of the Chancellor's foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Over time, courts of equity developed solid <a href="/wiki/Maxims_of_equity" title="Maxims of equity">principles</a>, especially under <a href="/wiki/John_Scott,_1st_Earl_of_Eldon" title="John Scott, 1st Earl of Eldon">Lord Eldon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> In the 19th century in England, and in <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Hotel_Co._v._Parrish" title="West Coast Hotel Co. v. Parrish">1937 in the U.S.</a>, the two systems were <a href="/wiki/Common_law#1870_through_20th_century,_and_the_procedural_merger_of_law_and_equity" title="Common law">merged</a>.
</p><p>In developing the common law, <a href="/wiki/Common_law#Contrasting_role_of_treatises_and_academic_writings_in_common_law_and_civil_law_systems" title="Common law">academic writings</a> have always played an important part, both to collect overarching principles from dispersed case law, and to argue for change. <a href="/wiki/William_Blackstone" title="William Blackstone">William Blackstone</a>, from around 1760, was the first scholar to collect, describe, and teach the common law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> But merely in describing, scholars who sought explanations and underlying structures slowly changed the way the law actually worked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_law">Religious law</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_law" title="Religious law">Religious law</a></div>
<p>Religious law is explicitly based on religious precepts. Examples include the Jewish <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Halakha</a> and Islamic <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a>—both of which translate as the "path to follow"—while Christian <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a> also survives in some church communities. Often the implication of religion for law is unalterability, because the word of God cannot be amended or legislated against by judges or governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> However, a thorough and detailed legal system generally requires human elaboration. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> has some law, and it acts as a source of further law through interpretation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Qiyas" title="Qiyas">Qiyas</a></i> (reasoning by analogy), <i><a href="/wiki/Ijma" title="Ijma">Ijma</a></i> (consensus) and <a href="/wiki/Precedent" title="Precedent">precedent</a>. This is mainly contained in a body of law and jurisprudence known as <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Fiqh</a> respectively. Another example is the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> or <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentateuch" title="Pentateuch">Pentateuch</a> or Five Books of Moses. This contains the basic code of Jewish law, which some Israeli communities choose to use. The <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Halakha</a> is a code of Jewish law that summarizes some of the Talmud's interpretations. Nevertheless, <a href="/wiki/Israeli_law" title="Israeli law">Israeli law</a> allows <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Litigant" title="Litigant">litigants</a> to use religious laws only if they choose. Canon law is only in use by members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canon_law_(Catholic_Church)" title="Canon law (Catholic Church)">Catholic Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canon_law">Canon law</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">Canon law</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graverat_titelblad_till_%22Kanonisk_r%C3%A4tt%22_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_93238.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2800" data-file-width="2162" decoding="async" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Graverat_titelblad_till_%22Kanonisk_r%C3%A4tt%22_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_93238.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Graverat_titelblad_till_%22Kanonisk_r%C3%A4tt%22_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_93238.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Graverat_titelblad_till_%22Kanonisk_r%C3%A4tt%22_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_93238.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Graverat_titelblad_till_%22Kanonisk_r%C3%A4tt%22_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_93238.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Graverat_titelblad_till_%22Kanonisk_r%C3%A4tt%22_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_93238.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Graverat_titelblad_till_%22Kanonisk_r%C3%A4tt%22_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_93238.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graverat_titelblad_till_%22Kanonisk_r%C3%A4tt%22_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_93238.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Canonici" title="Corpus Juris Canonici">Corpus Juris Canonici</a></i>, the fundamental collection of canon law for over 750 years</div></div></div>
<p>Canon law (from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>kanon</i>, a 'straight measuring rod, <a href="/wiki/Ruler" title="Ruler">ruler</a>') is a set of ordinances and regulations made by <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_jurisdiction" title="Ecclesiastical jurisdiction">ecclesiastical authority</a> (Church leadership), for the government of a Christian organisation or church and its members. It is the internal <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical" title="Ecclesiastical">ecclesiastical</a> law governing the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> (both the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">Eastern Catholic Churches</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodox</a> churches, and the individual national churches within the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The way that such church law is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legislative_power" title="Legislative power">legislated</a>, interpreted and at times <a href="/wiki/Court" title="Court">adjudicated</a> varies widely among these three bodies of churches. In all three traditions, a <a href="/wiki/Canon_(canon_law)" title="Canon (canon law)">canon</a> was originally<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-108">[108]</a></sup> a rule adopted by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_council" title="Church council">church council</a>; these canons formed the foundation of canon law.
</p><p>The Catholic Church has the oldest continuously functioning legal system in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">western world</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-White-110">[110]</a></sup> predating the evolution of modern European <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> and common law systems. The <a href="/wiki/1983_Code_of_Canon_Law" title="1983 Code of Canon Law">1983 Code of Canon Law</a> governs the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sui_juris" title="Sui juris">sui juris</a></i>. The Eastern Catholic Churches, which developed different disciplines and practices, are governed by the <i><a href="/wiki/Code_of_Canons_of_the_Eastern_Churches" title="Code of Canons of the Eastern Churches">Code of Canons of the Eastern Churches</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Canon_law_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Canon law of the Catholic Church">canon law of the Catholic Church</a> influenced the <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> during the medieval period<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> through its preservation of <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a> doctrine such as the <a href="/wiki/Presumption_of_innocence" title="Presumption of innocence">presumption of innocence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sharia_law">Sharia law</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="264" data-file-width="709" decoding="async" height="82" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png/220px-1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png/330px-1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png/440px-1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1879-Ottoman_Court-from-NYL.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A trial in the Ottoman Empire, 1879, when religious law applied under the <a href="/wiki/Mecelle" title="Mecelle">Mecelle</a></div></div></div>
<p>Until the 18th century, Sharia law was practiced throughout the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a> in a non-codified form, with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Mecelle" title="Mecelle">Mecelle</a> code in the 19th century being a first attempt at <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codifying</a> elements of Sharia law. Since the mid-1940s, efforts have been made, in country after country, to bring Sharia law more into line with modern conditions and conceptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AA_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-AA-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In modern times, the legal systems of many Muslim countries draw upon both civil and common law traditions as well as Islamic law and custom. The constitutions of certain Muslim states, such as Egypt and Afghanistan, recognise Islam as the religion of the state, obliging legislature to adhere to Sharia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Saudi Arabia recognises Quran as its constitution, and is governed on the basis of Islamic law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jurist_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jurist-117">[117]</a></sup> Iran has also witnessed a reiteration of Islamic law into its legal system after 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> During the last few decades, one of the fundamental features of the movement of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_resurgence" title="Islamic resurgence">Islamic resurgence</a> has been the call to restore the Sharia, which has generated a vast amount of literature and affected <a href="/wiki/Global_politics" title="Global politics">world politics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H1_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-H1-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_methods">Legal methods</span></h2>
<p>There are distinguished methods of legal reasoning (applying the law) and methods of interpreting (construing) the law. The former are <a href="/wiki/Legal_syllogism" title="Legal syllogism">legal syllogism</a>, which holds sway in civil law legal systems, <a href="/wiki/Analogy" title="Analogy">analogy</a>, which is present in common law legal systems, especially in the US, and argumentative theories that occur in both systems. The latter are different rules (directives) of legal interpretation such as directives of linguistic interpretation, teleological interpretation or systemic interpretation as well as more specific rules, for instance, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_rule" title="Golden rule">golden rule</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mischief_rule" title="Mischief rule">mischief rule</a>. There are also many other arguments and cannons of interpretation which altogether make <a href="/wiki/Statutory_interpretation" title="Statutory interpretation">statutory interpretation</a> possible.
</p><p>Law professor and former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">United States Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/Edward_H._Levi" title="Edward H. Levi">Edward H. Levi</a> noted that the "basic pattern of legal reasoning is reasoning by example"—that is, reasoning by comparing outcomes in cases resolving similar legal questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> In a U.S. Supreme Court case regarding procedural efforts taken by a debt collection company to avoid errors, Justice <a href="/wiki/Sonia_Sotomayor" title="Sonia Sotomayor">Sotomayor</a> cautioned that "legal reasoning is not a mechanical or strictly linear process".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jurimetrics" title="Jurimetrics">Jurimetrics</a> is the formal application of quantitative methods, especially <a href="/wiki/Probability_theory" title="Probability theory">probability</a> and <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a>, to legal questions. The use of statistical methods in court cases and law review articles has grown massively in importance in the last few decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_institutions">Legal institutions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:22em; ; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>It is a real unity of them all in one and the same person, made by covenant of every man with every man, in such manner as if every man should say to every man: I authorise and give up my right of governing myself to this man, or to this assembly of men, on this condition; that thou givest up, thy right to him, and authorise all his actions in like manner.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">Thomas Hobbes, <i>Leviathan</i>, <a class="external text" href="http://oregonstate.edu/instruct/phl302/texts/hobbes/leviathan-c.html#CHAPTERXVII" rel="nofollow">XVII</a></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The main institutions of law in industrialised countries are independent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Courts" title="Courts">courts</a>, representative parliaments, an accountable executive, the military and police, <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucratic</a> organisation, the <a href="/wiki/Legal_profession" title="Legal profession">legal profession</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civil society</a> itself.
